But <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The Chief Justice</small></span> says that statement in <em>McConnell</em> cannot be accepted at face value because we could not, consistent with precedent, have focused our First Amendment enquiry on whether "the speaker actually intended to affect an election."  <em>Ante</em>, at 14.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 17" name="FRdissent1.17" href="#FNdissent1.17">17</a></sup>  <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The Chief Justice</small></span> suggests it is more likely that the <em>McConnell</em> opinion inadvertently borrowed the language of "intended ... effect[s]," 540 U.&nbsp;S., at 206, from academic studies in the record of viewers' perceptions of the ads' purposes, <em>ante</em>, at 12.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 18" name="FRdissent1.18" href="#FNdissent1.18">18</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;If <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Chief Justice</small></span> were correct that <em>McConnell</em> made the constitutional application of 203 contingent on whether a corporation's "motives were pure," or its issue advocacy "subjective[ly] sincer[e]," <em>ante</em>, at 14 (internal quotation marks omitted), then I, too, might be inclined to reconsider <em>McConnell</em>'s language.  But <em>McConnell</em> did not do that.  It did not purport to draw constitutional lines based on the subjective motivations of corporations (or their principals) sponsoring political ads, but merely described our test for equivalence to express advocacy as resting on the ads' "electioneering purpose," which will be objectively apparent from those ads' content and context (as these cases and the examples cited in <em>McConnell </em>readily show).  We therefore held that 203 was not substantially overbroad because "the vast majority of ads clearly had such a purpose," and consequently could be regulated consistent with the First Amendment.  540 U.&nbsp;S., at 206.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;For that matter, if the studies to which <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Chief Justice</small></span> refers were now to inform our reading of <em>McConnell</em>, they would merely underscore the objective character of the proper way to determine whether 203 is constitutional as applied to a given ad.  The authors of those studies did not conduct discovery of the "actua[l] inten[tions]," <em>ante</em>, at 14, behind any ads; nor, to my knowledge, were the sponsors of campaign ads summoned before researchers to explain their motivations.  The studies merely confirmed that "reasonable people are ... able to discern between ads whose primary purpose is to support a candidate and those intended to provide information about a policy issue."  J. Krasno &amp; D. Seltz, Buying Time: Television Advertising in the 1998 Congressional Elections 9 (2000).  To be clear, I am not endorsing the precise methodology of those studies (and <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Chief Justice</small></span> is correct that we did not do so in <em>McConnell</em>, <em>ante</em>, at 13, n. 4); the point is only that the studies relied on a "reasonable" person's understanding of the ads' apparent purpose, and thus were no less objective than <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Chief Justice</small></span>'s own approach.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;A similarly mistaken fear of an unadministrable and speech-chilling subjective regime seems to underlie <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Chief Justice'</small></span>s unwillingness to acknowledge the part that consideration of an ad's context necessarily plays in any realistic assessment of its meaning.  A reasonable Wisconsinite watching or listening to WRTL's ads would likely ask and answer some obvious questions about their circumstances.  Is the group that sponsors these ads the same one publicly campaigning against Senator Feingold's reelection?  <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Chief Justice</small></span> says that this information is "beside the point," because WRTL's history of overt electioneering only "goes to [its] subjective intent."  <em>Ante</em>, at 18.  Did these "issue" ads begin appearing on the air during the election season, rather than at the time the filibuster "issue" was in fact being debated in the Senate?  This, too, is said to be irrelevant.  <em>Ante</em>, at 19.  And does the website to which WRTL's ads direct viewers contain material expressly advocating Senator Feingold's defeat?  This enquiry is dismissed as being "one step removed from the text of the ads themselves."  <em>Ante</em>, at 20.  But these questions are central to the meaning of the ads, and any reasonable person would take account of circumstances in coming to understand the object of WRTL's ad.  And why not?  Each of the contextual facts here can be established by an objective look at a public record; none requires a voter (or a litigant) to engage in discovery of evidence about WRTL's operations or internal communications, and none goes to a hidden state of mind.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;This refusal to see and hear what any listener to WRTL's ads would actually consider produces a rule no different in practice from the one adopted by the District Court, which declined to look beyond the "four corners" of the ads themselves.  466 F.&nbsp;Supp. 2d 195, 207 (DC 2006).  Although <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Chief Justice</small></span> ostensibly stops short of categorically foreclosing consideration of context, see <em>ante</em>, at 20, the application of his test here makes it difficult to see how relevant contextual evidence could ever be taken into account the way it was in <em>McConnell</em>,<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 19" name="FRdissent1.19" href="#FNdissent1.19">19</a></sup> and it is hard to imagine <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Chief Justice</small></span> would ever find an ad to be "susceptible of no reasonable interpretation other than as an appeal to vote for or against a specific candidate," <em>ante</em>, at 16, unless it contained words of express advocacy.  <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The Chief Justice</small></span> thus effectively reinstates the same toothless "magic words" criterion of regulable electioneering that led Congress to enact BCRA in the first place.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">C</p>
</h2><p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Third, it may be that the principal opinion rejects <em>McConnell </em>on the erroneous assumption that 203 flatly bans independent electioneering communications by a corporation.  <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The Chief Justice</small></span> argues that corporations must receive "the benefit of any doubt," <em>ante</em>, at 16,<em> </em>whenever we undertake the task of "separating ... political speech protected under the First Amendment from that which may be banned," <em>ante</em>, at 13.  But this is a fundamental misconception of the task at hand:  we have already held that it is "&nbsp;'simply wrong' to view [203] as a 'complete ban' on expression," because PAC financing provides corporations "with a constitutionally sufficient opportunity to engage in express advocacy."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 20" name="FRdissent1.20" href="#FNdissent1.20">20</a></sup>  <em>McConnell</em>, 540 U.&nbsp;S., at 203-204 (quoting <em>Beaumont</em>, 539 U.&nbsp;S., at 162).  Thus, a successful as-applied challenger to 203 should necessarily show, at the least, that it could not constitutionally be subjected to the administrative rules that govern a PAC's formation and operation.  See <em>id.</em>, at 163.  This would be an uphill fight, after our repeated affirmations that the PAC structure does not impose excessive burdens, <em>ibid.</em> (citing <em>National Right to Work Comm.</em>, 459 U.&nbsp;S., at 201-202), and WRTL has a particularly weak position on this point: it set up its own PAC long before the 2004 election, used it to campaign openly against Senator Feingold in the past, and could have raised noncorporate donations to it in the 2004 election cycle.  Any argument that establishing and maintaining a PAC is unconstitutionally burdensome for WRTL would thus likely be futile, and certainly should not prevail on WRTL's summary judgment motion.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;For that matter, even without the PAC alternative, it would be untrue that 203 "banned" WRTL from saying anything a genuine issue ad would say, for WRTL could have availed itself of either or both of the following additional options.  It is undisputed that WRTL's ads could have been broadcast lawfully in the runup to the election (and bankrolled from WRTL's general treasury) if Senator Feingold's name had been omitted and the Senator not otherwise singled out.  Since members of today's majority apparently view WRTL's broadcasts either as "genuine issue ad[s]," <em>ante</em>, at 16 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The Chief Justice</small></span>), or as "lobby[ing] Wisconsin voters concerning the filibustering of the President's judicial nominees," <em>ante</em>, at 2 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Scalia, J.,</small></span> concurring in part and concurring in judgment), a claim that omitting Senator Feingold's name would "ban" WRTL's message is specious.  Yet one searches my Brothers' opinions in vain for any persuasive reason why substituting the phrase "Contact your Senators" for the phrase "Contact Senators Feingold and Kohl" would have denied WRTL a constitutionally sufficient (and clearly lawful) alternative way to send its message.  If WRTL is to be believed when it claims that the issue was the point of the ads, it would have lost nothing by referring simply to the "Senators."</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Finally, the suggestion that 203 is a ban on political speech is belied by <em>MCFL</em>'s safe harbor for nonprofit advocacy corporations: under that rule, WRTL would have been free to attack Senator Feingold by name at any time with ads funded from its corporate treasury, if it had not also chosen to serve as a funnel for hundreds of thousands of dollars from other corporations.  Thus, what is called a "ban" on speech is a limit on the financing of electioneering broadcasts by entities that refuse to take advantage of the PAC structure but insist on acting as conduits from the campaign war chests of business corporations.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">D</p>
</h2><p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In sum, <em>McConnell</em> does not graft a subjective standard onto campaign regulation, the context of campaign advertising cannot sensibly be ignored, and 203 is not a ban on speech.  What cannot be gainsaid, in any event, is that in treating these subjects as it does, the operative opinion produces the result of overruling <em>McConnell</em>'s holding on 203, less than four years in the Reports.  Anyone who doubts that need merely ask what the law would have been if, back in 2003, this Court had held 203 facially unconstitutional.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;BCRA's definition of "electioneering communication," which identifies the communications regulable under 203, includes a backup to be used if the primary definition "is held to be constitutionally insufficient by final judicial decision to support the regulation provided herein."  2 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 434(f)(3)(A)(ii) (2000 ed., Supp. IV).  If this should occur, "electioneering communication" is to be defined as </p>
<blockquote><p>"any broadcast, cable, or satellite communication which promotes or supports a candidate for that office, or attacks or opposes a candidate for that office (regardless of whether the communication expressly advocates a vote for or against a candidate) and which also is suggestive of no plausible meaning other than an exhortation to vote for or against a specific candidate."  <em>Ibid.</em></p>
</blockquote><p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;This backup sounds familiar because it is essentially identical to <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The Chief Justice</small></span>'s test for evaluating an as-applied challenge to the original definition of "electioneering communication": regulation is permissible only if the communication is "susceptible of no reasonable interpretation other than as an appeal to vote for or against a specific candidate," <em>ante</em>, at 16.  Thus does the principal opinion institute the very standard that would have prevailed if the Court formally overruled <em>McConnell</em>.  There is neither a theoretical nor a practical basis to claim that <em>McConnell</em>'s treatment of 203 survives.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">E</p>
</h2><p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The price of <em>McConnell'</em>s<em> </em>demise as authority on 203 seems to me to be a high one.  The Court (and, I think, the country) loses when important precedent is overruled without good reason, and there is no justification for departing from our usual rule of <em>stare decisis</em> here.  The same combination of alternatives that was available to corporations affected by <em>McConnell</em> in 2003 is available today: WRTL could have run a newspaper ad, could have paid for the broadcast ads through its PAC, could have established itself as an <em>MCFL</em> organization free of corporate money, and could have said "call your Senators" instead of naming Senator Feingold in its ads broadcasted just before the election.  Nothing in the related law surrounding 203 has changed in any way, let alone in any way that undermines <em>McConnell</em>'s<em> </em>rationale.  See <em>Planned Parenthood of Southeastern Pa.</em> v. <em>Casey</em>, 505 U.&nbsp;S. 833, 854-855 (1992).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Nor can any serious argument be made that <em>McConnell</em>'s holding has been "unworkable in practice."  <em>Allied-Signal, Inc.</em> v. <em>Director, Div. of Taxation</em>, 504 U.&nbsp;S. 768, 783 (1992) (internal quotation marks omitted)<em>.</em>  <em>McConnell </em>validated a clear rule resting on mostly bright-line conditions, and there is no indication that the statute has been difficult to apply.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 21" name="FRdissent1.21" href="#FNdissent1.21">21</a></sup>  Although WRTL contends that the as-applied remedy has proven to be "[i]nadequate" because such challenges cannot be litigated quickly enough to avoid being mooted, Brief for Appellee 65-66, nothing prevents an advertiser from obtaining a preliminary injunction if it can qualify for one, and WRTL does not point to any evidence that district courts have been unable to rule on any such matters in a timely way.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Finally, it goes without saying that nothing has changed about the facts.  In Justice Frankfurter's words, they demonstrate a threat to "the integrity of our electoral process," <em>Automobile Workers</em>, 352 U.&nbsp;S., at 570, which for a century now Congress has repeatedly found to be imperiled by corporate, and later union, money: witness the Tillman Act, Taft-Hartley, FECA, and BCRA.  See Part II, <em>supra</em>.  <em>McConnell </em>was our latest decision vindicating clear and reasonable boundaries that Congress has drawn to limit "&nbsp;'the corrosive and distorting effects of immense aggregations of wealth,'&nbsp;" 540 U.&nbsp;S., at 205 (quoting <em>Austin</em>, 494 U.&nbsp;S., at 660), and the decision could claim the justification of ongoing fact as well as decisional history in recognizing Congress's authority to protect the integrity of elections from the distortion of corporate and union funds.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;After today, the ban on contributions by corporations and unions and the limitation on their corrosive spending when they enter the political arena are open to easy circumvention, and the possibilities for regulating corporate and union campaign money are unclear.  The ban on contributions will mean nothing much, now that companies and unions can save candidates the expense of advertising directly, simply by running "issue ads" without express advocacy, or by funneling the money through an independent corporation like WRTL.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;But the understanding of the voters and the Congress that this kind of corporate and union spending seriously jeopardizes the integrity of democratic government will remain.  The facts are too powerful to be ignored, and further efforts at campaign finance reform will come.  It is only the legal landscape that now is altered, and it may be that today's departure from precedent will drive further reexamination of the constitutional analysis: of the distinction between contributions and expenditures, or the relation between spending and speech, which have given structure to our thinking since <em>Buckley</em> itself was decided.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;I cannot tell what the future will force upon us, but I respectfully dissent from this judgment today.</p>
<p></p><hr>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNopinion1.4" href="#FRopinion1.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;This is particularly true given that the methodology, data, and conclusions of the two studies were the subject of serious dispute among the District Court judges.  Compare <em>McConnell </em>v. <em>FEC</em>, 251 F.&nbsp;Supp. 2d 176, 307-312 (DC 2003) (opinion of Henderson, J.) (stating that the studies were flawed and of limited evidentiary value), with <em>id., </em>at 585, 583-588 (opinion of Kollar-Kotelly, J.) (finding the studies generally credible, but stating that "I am troubled by the fact that coders in both studies were asked questions regarding their own perceptions of the advertisements' purposes, and that [some of] these perceptions were later recoded" by study supervisors).  Nothing in this Court's opinion in <em>McConnell</em> suggests it was resolving the sharp disagreements about the evidentiary record in this respect.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 5" name="FNopinion1.5" href="#FRopinion1.5">5</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Consider what happened in these cases.  The District Court permitted extensive discovery on the assumption that WRTL's intent was relevant.  As a result, the defendants deposed WRTL's executive director, its legislative director, its political action committee director, its lead communications consultant, and one of its fundraisers.  WRTL also had to turn over many documents related to its operations, plans, and finances.  Such litigation constitutes a severe burden on political speech.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNopinion1.6" href="#FRopinion1.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;For these reasons, we cannot agree with <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Souter'</small></span>s assertion that "anyone who heard the Feingold ads ... would know that WRTL's message was to vote against Feingold."  <em>Post, </em>at 23.  The dissent supports this assertion by likening WRTL's ads to the "Jane Doe" example identified in <em>McConnell</em> v. <em>Federal Election Comm'n, </em>540 U.&nbsp;S. 93 (2003).  But that ad "condemned Jane Doe's record on a particular issue."  <em>Post, </em>at 23 (internal quotation marks omitted).  WRTL's ads do not do so; they instead take a position on the filibuster issue and exhort constituents to contact Senators Feingold and Kohl to advance that position.  Indeed, one would not even know from the ads whether Senator Feingold supported or opposed filibusters.  <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Souter </small></span>is confident Wisconsinites independently knew Senator Feingold's position on filibusters, but we think that confidence misplaced.  A prominent study found, for example, that during the 2000 election cycle, 85 percent of respondents to a survey were not even able to name at least one candidate for the House of Representatives in their own district.  See Inter-university Consortium for Political and Social Research, American National Election Study, 2000: Pre- and Post-Election Survey 243 (N. Burns et&nbsp;al. eds. 2002) online at http://www.icpsr.umich.edu/ cocoon/ICPSR/STUDY/03131.xml (as visited June 22, 2007, and available in Clerk of Court's case file).  </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 7" name="FNopinion1.7" href="#FRopinion1.7">7</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Scalia</small></span> thinks our test impermissibly vague.  See <em>post</em>, at 11-12 (opinion concurring in part and concurring in judgment).  As should be evident, we agree with <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Scalia </small></span>on the imperative for clarity in this area; that is why our test affords protection unless an ad is susceptible of <em>no reasonable interpretation</em> other than as an appeal to vote for or against a specific candidate.  It is why we emphasize that (1) there can be no free-ranging intent-and-effect test; (2) there generally should be no discovery or inquiry into the sort of "contextual" factors highlighted by the FEC and intervenors; (3) discussion of issues cannot be banned merely because the issues might be relevant to an election; and (4) in a debatable case, the tie is resolved in favor of protecting speech.  And keep in mind this test is only triggered if the speech meets the brightline requirements of BCRA 203 in the first place.  <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Scalia</small></span>'s criticism of our test is all the more confusing because he accepts WRTL's proposed three-prong test as "clear."  <em>Post, </em>at 17.  We do not think our test any vaguer than WRTL's, and it is more protective of political speech.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Scalia </small></span>also asserts that our test conflicts with <em>Buckley</em> v. <em>Valeo, </em>424 U.&nbsp;S. 1 (1976) (<em>per curiam</em>)<em>.  Post, </em>at 13-16.  The <em>Buckley </em>Court confronted a statute restricting "any expenditure ... relative to a clearly identified candidate."  424 U.&nbsp;S., at 42 (internal quotation marks omitted).  To avoid vagueness concerns, this Court first narrowed the statute to cover only expenditures expressly "advocating the election or defeat of a candidate"--using the so-called "magic words" of express advocacy.  <em>Ibid.</em> (internal quotation marks omitted).  The Court then proceeded to strike down the newly narrowed statute under strict scrutiny on the ground that its reach was not broad enough.  <em>Id.</em>, at 44.  From this, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Scalia</small></span> concludes that "[i]f a permissible test short of the magic-words test existed, <em>Buckley</em> would surely have adopted it."  <em>Post</em>, at 14.  We are not so sure.  The question in <em>Buckley</em> was how a particular statutory provision could be construed to avoid vagueness concerns, not what the constitutional standard for clarity was in the abstract, divorced from specific statutory language.  <em>Buckley</em>'s intermediate step of statutory construction on the way to its constitutional holding does not dictate a constitutional test.  The <em>Buckley</em> Court's "express advocacy restriction was an endpoint of statutory interpretation, not a first principle of constitutional law."  <em>McConnell</em>, 540 U.&nbsp;S., at 190.  And despite <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Scalia</small></span>'s claim to the contrary, our citation of <em>Buckley </em>along with other decisions in rejecting an intent-and-effect test does not force us to adopt (or reject) <em>Buckley</em>'s statutory construction as a constitutional test.  </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 8" name="FNopinion1.8" href="#FRopinion1.8">8</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Nothing in <em>McConnell</em>'s statement that the "vast majority" of issue ads broadcast in the periods preceding federal elections had an "electioneering purpose" forecloses this conclusion.  540 U.&nbsp;S., at 206.  Courts do not resolve unspecified as-applied challenges in the course of resolving a facial attack, so <em>McConnell</em> could not have settled the issue we address today.  See <em>Members of City Council of Los Angeles</em> v. <em>Taxpayers for Vincent</em>, 466 U.&nbsp;S. 789, 803, n. 22 (1984) ("The fact that [a law] is capable of valid applications does not necessarily mean that it is valid as applied to these litigants").  Indeed, <em>WRTL I</em> confirmed as much.  546 U.&nbsp;S., at 411-412.  By the same token, in deciding this as-applied challenge, we have no occasion to revisit <em>McConnell</em>'s conclusion that the statute is not facially overbroad.</p>
<p>

The "vast majority" language, moreover, is beside the point.  The <em>McConnell</em> Court did not find that a "vast majority" of the issue ads considered were the functional equivalent of direct advocacy.  Rather, it found that such ads had an "electioneering purpose."  For the reasons we have explained, "purpose" is not the appropriate test for distinguishing between genuine issue ads and the functional equivalent of express campaign advocacy.  See <em>supra</em>, at 14-15.  In addition, the "vast majority" statement was not necessary to the Court's facial holding in <em>McConnell</em>.  The standard required for a statute to survive an overbreadth challenge is not that the "vast majority" of a statute's applications be legitimate.  "[B]road language . . . unnecessary to the Court's decision ... cannot be considered binding authority."  <em>Kastigar</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 406 U.&nbsp;S. 441, 454-455 (1972).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 9" name="FNopinion1.9" href="#FRopinion1.9">9</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The dissent stresses a number of points that, while not central to our decision, nevertheless merit a response.  First, the dissent overstates its case when it asserts that the "PAC alternative" gives corporations a constitutionally sufficient outlet to speak.  See <em>post, </em>at 30.  PACs impose well-documented and onerous burdens, particularly on small nonprofits.  See <em>MCFL</em>, 479 U.&nbsp;S. 238, 253-255 (1986) (plurality opinion).  <em>McConnell</em> did conclude that segregated funds "provid[e] corporations and unions with a constitutionally sufficient opportunity to engage in express advocacy" and its functional equivalent, 540 U.&nbsp;S., at 203, but that holding did not extend beyond functional equivalents--and if it did, the PAC option would justify regulation of all corporate speech, a proposition we have rejected, see <em>Bellotti</em>, 435 U.&nbsp;S., at 777-778.  Second, the response that a speaker should just take out a newspaper ad, or use a website, rather than complain that it cannot speak through a broadcast communication, see <em>post, </em>at 18-19, 33, is too glib.  Even assuming for the sake of argument that the possibility of using a different medium of communication has relevance in determining the permissibility of a limitation on speech, newspaper ads and websites are not reasonable alternatives to broadcast speech in terms of impact and effectiveness.  See <em>McConnell </em>v. <em>FEC</em>, 251 F.&nbsp;Supp. 2d, at 569-573, 646 (Kollar-Kotelly, J.).  Third, we disagree with the dissent's view that corporations can still speak by changing what they say to avoid mentioning candidates, <em>post, </em>at 30-31.  That argument is akin to telling Cohen that he cannot wear his jacket because he is free to wear one that says "I disagree with the draft," cf. <em>Cohen</em> v. <em>California</em>, 403 U.&nbsp;S. 15 (1971), or telling 44 Liquormart that it can advertise so long as it avoids mentioning prices, cf. <em>44 Liquormart, Inc.</em> v. <em>Rhode Island</em>, 517 U.&nbsp;S. 484 (1996).  Such notions run afoul of "the fundamental rule of protection under the First Amendment, that a speaker has the autonomy to choose the content of his own message."  <em>Hurley</em> v. <em>Irish-American Gay, Lesbian and Bisexual Group of Boston, Inc.</em>, 515 U.&nbsp;S. 557, 573 (1995).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 10" name="FNopinion1.10" href="#FRopinion1.10">10</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The interest recognized in <em>Austin</em> stems from a concern that "&nbsp;'[t]he resources in the treasury of a business corporation . . . are not an indication of popular support for the corporation's political ideas.'&nbsp;"  <em>Austin</em> v. <em>Michigan Chamber of Commerce</em>, 494 U.&nbsp;S. 652, 659 (1990) (alteration in original).  Some of WRTL's <em>amici</em> contend that this interest is not implicated here because of WRTL's status as a nonprofit advocacy organization.  They assert that "[s]peech by nonprofit advocacy groups on behalf of their members does not 'corrupt' candidates or 'distort' the political marketplace," and that "[n]onprofit advocacy groups funded by individuals are readily distinguished from for-profit corporations funded by general treasuries."  Brief for Family Research Council et&nbsp;al. as <em>Amici Curiae </em>3, 4.  Cf. <em>MCFL,</em> 479 U.&nbsp;S., at 264.  We do not pass on this argument in this as-applied challenge because WRTL's funds for its ads were not derived solely from individual contributions.  See Brief for Appellant FEC 11.</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3><br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNconcurrence2.1" href="#FRconcurrence2.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Whitlock, Satirist Continues to Prove Himself a Royal Pain, Washington Post, Apr. 26, 2005, pp. C1, C8.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNconcurrence2.2" href="#FRconcurrence2.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;BCRA also includes a backup definition of "electioneering communication" that will take effect in the event the primary definition is "held to be constitutionally insufficient ... to support the regulation provided herein."  2 U. S. C. 434(f)(3)(A)(ii) (2000 ed., Supp. IV).  This defines "electioneering communication" as "any broadcast, cable, or satellite communication which promotes or supports a candidate for [a federal] office, or attacks or opposes a candidate for that office (regardless of whether the communication expressly advocates a vote for or against a candidate) and which also is suggestive of no plausible meaning other than an exhortation to vote for or against a specific candidate."  <em>Ibid.</em></p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNconcurrence2.3" href="#FRconcurrence2.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In <em>Federal Election Comm'n</em> v. <em>Massachusetts Citizens for Life, Inc.</em>, 479 U. S. 238, 248 (1986) <em>(MCFL)</em>, we addressed the pre-BCRA version of 2 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 441b, which was interpreted to ban corporate treasury expenditures for <em>express advocacy</em> in connection with federal elections.  We held that, "[r]egardless of whether th[e] concern [for unfair advantage to organizations that amass great wealth] is adequate to support application of 441b to commercial enterprises, <em>a question not before us</em>, that justification" did not support application of the statute to the nonprofit organization that brought the challenge in <em>MCFL</em>.  479 U.&nbsp;S., at 263 (emphasis added).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNconcurrence2.4" href="#FRconcurrence2.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The dissent asserts that <em>Austin</em> was faithful to <em>Bellotti</em>'s principles, to prove which it quotes a footnote in <em>Bellotti</em> leaving open the possibility that independent expenditures by corporations might someday be demonstrated to beget <em>quid-pro-quo</em> corruption. <em>Post</em>, at 12, n.&nbsp;6 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Souter, J</small></span>.) (quoting <em>Bellotti</em>, 435 U.&nbsp;S., at 788, n. 26).  That someday has never come.  No one seriously believes that <em>independent</em> expenditures could possibly give rise to <em>quid-pro-quo</em> corruption without being subject to regulation as <em>coordinated</em> expenditures.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 5" name="FNconcurrence2.5" href="#FRconcurrence2.5">5</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;J<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>ustice Alito</small></span>'s concurrence at least hints that the principal opinion's test <em>may</em> impermissibly chill speech, and offers to reconsider <em>McConnell</em>'s holding "<em>[i]f</em> it turns out that the implementation of the as-applied standard set out in the [principal opinion] impermissibly chills political speech."  <em>Post</em>, at 1 (emphasis added).  The wait-and-see approach makes no sense and finds no support in our cases.  How will we know that would-be speakers have been chilled and have not spoken?  If a tree <em>does not</em> fall in the forest, can we hear the sound it would have made had it fallen?  Our normal practice is to assess <em>ex ante</em> the risk that a standard will have an impermissible chilling effect on First Amendment protected speech.  <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Alito</small></span> seemed to recognize that as recently as, well, today.  In another opinion released this morning, he finds that a proposed test for censoring student speech "can easily be manipulated in dangerous ways," wherefore he "would reject it <em>before such abuse occurs</em>."  <em>Morse</em> v. <em>Frederick</em>, <em>ante</em>, at 2 (concurring opinion) (emphasis added).  I would accord the core First Amendment speech at issue here at least the same respect he accords speech in the classroom.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNconcurrence2.6" href="#FRconcurrence2.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The principal opinion claims that its test is no more vague than WRTL's test.  See <em>ante</em>, at 21, n.&nbsp;7.  I disagree.  WRTL's test requires yes or no answers to a series of precise and focused questions: Does the ad take a position on a legislative matter?  Does it mention the election?  Does it expressly say the candidate is wrong for the office?  A group of children--indeed, even a group of college students--could answer these questions with great consistency.  The principal opinion's test, by contrast, hinges on assessment of the reasonableness of a determination that something does not constitute advocacy of the election or defeat of a candidate.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 7" name="FNconcurrence2.7" href="#FRconcurrence2.7">7</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The same must be said, I think, of the test proposed by the principal opinion.  While its coverage is not entirely clear, it would apparently protect even <em>McConnell</em>'s<em> </em>paradigmatic example of the functional equivalent of express advocacy--the so-called "Jane Doe ad," which "condemned Jane Doe's record on a particular issue before exhorting viewers to 'call Jane Doe and tell her what you think,'&nbsp;" 540 U.&nbsp;S., at 126-127.  Indeed, it at least arguably protects the most "striking" example of a so-called sham issue ad in the <em>McConnell</em> record, the notorious "Yellowtail ad," which accused Bill Yellowtail of striking his wife and then urged listeners to call him and "[t]ell him to support family values."  <em>Id.</em>, at 193-194, n. 78 (internal quotation marks omitted).  The claim that 203 on its face does not reach a substantial amount of speech protected under the principal opinion's test--and that the test is therefore compatible with <em>McConnell</em>--seems to me indefensible.  Indeed, the principal opinion's attempt at distinguishing <em>McConnell</em> is unpersuasive enough, and the change in the law it works is substantial enough, that seven Justices of this Court, having widely divergent views concerning the constitutionality of the restrictions at issue, agree that the opinion effectively overrules <em>McConnell</em> without saying so.  See <em>post</em>, at 24-25 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Souter, J.</small></span>, dissenting).  This faux judicial restraint is judicial obfuscation.  </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 8" name="FNconcurrence2.8" href="#FRconcurrence2.8">8</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Kennedy</small></span>'s opinion in <em>McConnell</em> explained why the possibility of corporations' funding speech out of a PAC does not save the statute from constitutional infirmity.  See 540 U.&nbsp;S., at 330-333.  <em>McConnell</em>'s rejection of those arguments rested, of course, upon the assumption that for non-PAC genuine issue ads as-applied challenges would be available.  See<em> id.</em>, at 207; <em>WRTL I,</em> 546 U.&nbsp;S. 410, 411-412 (2006) <em>(per curiam)</em>.  The discussion today shows that to be mistaken.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The dissent asserts, <em>post</em>, at 31, that there is no reason "why substituting the phrase 'Contact your Senators' for the phrase 'Contact Senators Feingold and Kohl' would have denied WRTL a constitutionally sufficient ... alternative."  Surely that is not so.  The purpose of the ad was to put political pressure upon Senator Feingold to change his position on the filibuster--not only through the constituents who accepted the invitation to contact him, but also through the very existence of an ad bringing to the public's attention that he, Senator Feingold, stood athwart the allowance of a vote on judicial nominees.  (Unlike the principal opinion, I think that the fair import of the ad in context.)  </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 9" name="FNconcurrence2.9" href="#FRconcurrence2.9">9</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>Seminole Tribe of Fla.</em> v. <em>Florida</em>, 517 U.&nbsp;S. 44 (1996) (overruling <em>Pennsylvania</em> v. <em>Union Gas Co.</em>, 491 U.&nbsp;S. 1 (1989)); <em>Adarand Constructors, Inc.</em> v. <em>Pe&amp;nacute;a</em>, 515 U.&nbsp;S. 200 (1995) (overruling in part <em>Metro Broadcasting, Inc.</em> v. <em>FCC</em>, 497 U.&nbsp;S. 547 (1990)); <em>United States</em> v. <em>Dixon</em>, 509 U.&nbsp;S. 688 (1993) (overruling <em>Grady</em> v. <em>Corbin</em>, 495 U.&nbsp;S. 508 (1990)); <em>Payne</em> v. <em>Tennessee</em>, 501 U.&nbsp;S. 808 (1991) (overruling <em>South Carolina</em> v. <em>Gathers</em>, 490 U.&nbsp;S. 805 (1989), and <em>Booth</em> v. <em>Maryland</em>, 482 U.&nbsp;S. 496 (1987)); <br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3><br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNdissent1.1" href="#FRdissent1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Substantially for the reasons stated by the Court, <em>ante</em>, at 7-10, I believe these cases are justiciable.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNdissent1.2" href="#FRdissent1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Between candidates, political action committees, interest groups, and national, state, and local parties, spending on the 2004 state and federal elections exceeded $4 billion.  K. Patterson, Spending in the 2004 Election, in Financing the 2004 Election 68, 71, tbl. 3-1 (D. Magleby, A. Corrado, &amp; K. Patterson eds. 2006).  Congressional campaigns spent over $1 billion in 2004, <em>id.</em>, at 75, tbl. 3-4, state legislative candidates raised three-quarters of a billion dollars in the 2003-2004 election cycle, The Institute on Money in State Politics, State Elections Overview 2004, p. 2 (2005), available at http://www.followthe money.org/press/Reports/200601041.pdf (all Internet materials as visited June 20, 2007, and available in Clerk of Court's case file), and gubernatorial candidates raised over $200 million, <em>id.</em>, at 6.  State judicial campaigns have become flush with cash as well, with state supreme court candidates raising over $30 million in the 2005-2006 cycle.  Sample et&nbsp;al., The New Politics of Judicial Elections 2006, p. 16 (2007), available at http://www.justiceatstake.org/files/NewPoliticsof JudicialElections2006.pdf.  In a single 2004 judicial election in Illinois, the candidates raised a breathtaking $9.3 million, an amount the winner called "&nbsp;'obscene.'&nbsp;"  The Justice-elect wondered, "&nbsp;'How can people have faith in the system?'&nbsp;"  Moyer &amp; Brandenburg, Big Money and Special Interests are Warping Judicial Elections, Legal Times, Oct. 9, 2006, p. 50 (quoting Justice Lloyd Karmeier of the Illinois Supreme Court).  According to polling data, the fear that people will lose trust in the system is well founded.  With respect to judicial elections, a context in which the influence of campaign contributions is most troubling, a recent poll of business leaders revealed that about four in five thought that campaign contributions have at least "some influence" on judges' decisions, while 90 percent are at least "somewhat concerned" that "[c]ampaign contributions and political pressure will make judges accountable to politicians and special interest groups instead of the law and the Constitution."  Zogby International, Attitudes and Views of American Business Leaders on State Judicial Elections and Political Contributions to Judges 4-5 (May 2007), available at http://www. ced.org/docs/report/report_2007judicial_survey.pdf.  People have similar feelings about other elected officials.  See M. Mellman &amp; R. Wirthlin, Public Views of Party Soft Money, in Inside the Campaign Finance Battle 266-269 (A. Corrado, T. Mann, &amp; T. Potter eds. 2003) (hereinafter Mellman &amp; Wirthlin); see also <em>infra</em>, at 4-5.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNdissent1.3" href="#FRdissent1.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Perhaps the President's call was inspired by the accusations from his own 1904 Democratic opponent, Judge Alton B. Parker, that the Republican camp accepted corporate campaign contributions intended to buy influence.  See A. Corrado, Money and Politics: A History of Federal Campaign Finance Law, in A. Corrado, T. Mann, D. Ortiz, &amp; T. Potter, The New Campaign Finance Sourcebook 7, 10-11 (2005) (hereinafter Campaign Finance Sourcebook).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNdissent1.4" href="#FRdissent1.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;A bill along similar lines had been unsuccessfully introduced years earlier by Senator William Chandler, a New Hampshire Republican whom the railroad interests helped defeat in 1900.  See Mutch 4-6 (discussing the unlikely alliance between Chandler, a radical Republican, and Senator Benjamin Tillman, a South Carolina Democrat who ultimately succeeded in enacting the law that carries his name).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 5" name="FNdissent1.5" href="#FRdissent1.5">5</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Taft-Hartley also specified that the prohibition extends to primary elections, 61 Stat. 159, an extension that had been thought likely to exceed the authority of Congress under Art. I, 4 of the Constitution until our decision in <em>United States</em> v. <em>Classic</em>, 313 U.&nbsp;S. 299, 317 (1941).  See H. Rep. No. 2093, 78th Cong., 2d Sess., 8-9 (1945) (discussing the significance of <em>Classic</em>).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNdissent1.6" href="#FRdissent1.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;FECA also validated corporate and union spending on internal communications and nonpartisan activities designed to promote voting.  See 86 Stat. 10 ("[T]he phrase 'contribution or expenditure' . . . shall not include communications by a corporation to its stockholders and their families, or by a labor organization to its members and their families on any subject [or] nonpartisan registration and get-out-the-vote campaigns by a corporation aimed at its stockholders and their families or by a labor organization aimed at its members and their families").  "&nbsp;'If an organization ... believes that certain candidates pose a threat to its well-being or the well-being of its members or stockholders, it should be able to get its views to those members or stockholders. . . . Both union members and stockholders have the right to expect this expert guidance.'&nbsp;"  <em>Pipefitters</em>, 407 U.&nbsp;S., at 431, n. 42 (quoting 117 Cong. Rec. 43380 (statement of Rep. Hansen)).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 7" name="FNdissent1.7" href="#FRdissent1.7">7</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Compare <em>Austin</em> v. <em>Michigan Chamber of Commerce</em>, 494 U.&nbsp;S. 652, 664 (1990) (First Amendment does not protect a nonprofit corporation from expenditure limits if the corporation accepts corporate and union contributions, lest corporations and unions readily "circumvent" restrictions on their own election spending "by funneling money through" nonprofits)<em>.</em>  <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Scalia</small></span> asserts that <em>Austin </em>"strayed far from" the principles we announced in <em>First Nat. Bank of Boston</em> v. <em>Bellotti</em>, 435 U.&nbsp;S. 765 (1978).  <em>Ante</em>, at 7 (opinion concurring in part and concurring in judgment).  <em>Bellotti</em>, however, concerned corporate spending in connection with a referendum, and we went out of our way in that case to avoid casting any doubt upon the constitutionality of limiting corporate expenditures during candidate elections.  We said:</p>
<p>

"The overriding concern behind the enactment of [the federal restrictions on corporate contributions and expenditures] was the problem of corruption of elected representatives through the creation of political debts.  The importance of the governmental interest in preventing this occurrence has never been doubted.  The case before us presents no comparable problem, and our consideration of a corporation's right to speak on issues of general public interest implies no comparable right in the quite different context of participation in a political campaign for election to public office.  Congress might well be able to demonstrate the existence of a danger of real or apparent corruption in independent expenditures by corporations to influence candidate elections."  435 U.&nbsp;S., at 788, n. 26 (citations omitted).</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Eight years before <em>Austin</em>, we unanimously reaffirmed that <em>Bellotti</em> "specifically pointed out that in elections of candidates to public office, unlike in referenda on issues of general public interest, there may well be a threat of real or apparent corruption."  <em>Federal Election Comm'n</em> v. <em>National Right to Work Comm.</em>, 459 U.&nbsp;S. 197, 210, n. 7 (1982).  Then, four years later, in <em>MCFL</em>, we also noted that an expenditure limit offering corporations a PAC alternative is "distinguishable from the complete foreclosure of any opportunity for political speech" that we addressed in <em>Bellotti</em>.  479 U.&nbsp;S., at 259, n.&nbsp;12.  So <em>Austin </em>did not "stra[y]" from <em>Bellotti</em>, <em>ante</em>, at 7 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Scalia, J.</small></span>);<em> </em>the reasons <em>Bellotti </em>was not controlling in <em>Austin </em>had been clearly foreshadowed in <em>Bellotti </em>itself and confirmed repeatedly in our decisions leading up to <em>Austin</em>.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 8" name="FNdissent1.8" href="#FRdissent1.8">8</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Or this example from a Texas district where Democrat Nick Lampson challenged incumbent Republican Steve Stockman, and where the AFL-CIO ran the following advertisement in September and October of 1996:</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"&nbsp;'[Narrator] What's important to America's families?  [Middle-aged man] "My pension is very important because it will provide a significant amount of my income when I retire."  [Narrator] And where do the candidates stand?  Congressman Steve Stockman voted to make it easier for corporations to raid employee pension funds.  Nick Lampson opposes that plan.  He supports new safeguards to protect employee pension funds.  When it comes to your pension, there is a difference.  Call and find out.'&nbsp;"  <em>McConnell</em> v. <em>FEC</em>, 251 F. Supp. 2d 176, 201 (DC 2003) <em>(per curiam) </em>(emphasis deleted; brackets in original).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 10" name="FNdissent1.10" href="#FRdissent1.10">10</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Senate Committee was not alone in its concerns.  In Wisconsin, for example, the Governor's Blue-Ribbon Commission on Campaign Finance Reform reported:</p>
<p>

"Especially beginning in 1996, issue advocacy during the campaign season dramatically expanded in Wisconsin.</p>
<blockquote><p align="CENTER">.....</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"The Commission concludes that, in each of these cases, the expenditures were clearly campaign-oriented activities.  They were quite clearly designed to influence the electoral process.  They were focused either on electing or defeating a candidate.  The Commission bases this conclusion on the following points:</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"Although those paying for the activities claimed they were aimed solely at educating voters on the issues, they each mentioned the names of candidates for office.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"They occurred only when election races were in progress that involved a contest between an incumbent and a challenger.  When the election was over, the activities ended.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"The activity has occurred after legislative sessions when the issues about which advocacy was occurring were not being deliberated by the legislature.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"The activity occurred in campaign season, between the candidate's filing for candidacy and election time.  Advertisements of this sort have tended to occur at virtually no other time.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"The activity involved the electronic media, mass mailings, or centrally located telephone banks.</p>
</blockquote><blockquote><p align="CENTER">.....</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"The explosive growth of campaign-based advocacy, without even disclosure of its activities and funding sources, poses a grave risk to the integrity of elections. It has created a two-tiered campaign process: one, based in candidates and political parties, which is tightly regulated and controlled; the other, based in interest group activity under the guise of 'issue advocacy' but actually quite clearly election-focused, which lies beyond accountability."  1 Governor's Blue-Ribbon Commission on Campaign Finance Reform, State of Wisconsin: Report of the Commission, available at http://www.lafollette.wisc.edu/campaign_reform/final. htm.</p>
</blockquote>
<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 11" name="FNdissent1.11" href="#FRdissent1.11">11</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Campaign finance laws also continue to provide several specific exemptions from the general prohibition on corporate election-related spending, including communications "on any subject" with stockholders and certain personnel, as well as "nonpartisan registration and get-out-the-vote campaigns" similarly aimed at shareholders and personnel.  441b(b)(2); see also n.&nbsp;6, <em>supra</em>.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 12" name="FNdissent1.12" href="#FRdissent1.12">12</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;To the extent these facts are disputed, we must view them in the light most favorable to the Federal Election Commission and the intervenor-defendants, since the District Court granted WRTL's motion for summary judgment.  See <em>Pennsylvania State Police</em> v. <em>Suders</em>, 542 U.&nbsp;S. 129, 134 (2004).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 13" name="FNdissent1.13" href="#FRdissent1.13">13</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;These quotations are taken from the "Wedding" ad, although the relevant language in all of the ads is virtually identical.  See <em>ante</em>, at 4-6, and nn. 2-3 (principal opinion).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 14" name="FNdissent1.14" href="#FRdissent1.14">14</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See, <em>e.g.</em>, Hearing before the Subcommittee on the Constitution, Civil Rights and Property Rights of the Senate Committee on the Judiciary, 108th Congress, 1st Sess., 5-7 (2003) (statement of Sen. Feingold).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 15" name="FNdissent1.15" href="#FRdissent1.15">15</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See Gilbert, 3 Seeking Feingold Seat Attack Him on Judges Issue, Milwaukee Journal Sentinel, Nov. 18, 2003, App. 70-76 ("In Wisconsin, the three Republicans vying to take on Senate Democrat Russ Feingold are attacking him on judges and assert the controversy resonates with voters").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 16" name="FNdissent1.16" href="#FRdissent1.16">16</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;That the ads purported to target Senator Kohl as well as Senator Feingold is of little import; since the ads would have run during the peak of the 2004 campaign, the audience's focus would naturally fall more heavily on Senator Feingold (who was up for reelection) rather than Senator Kohl (who was not).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 17" name="FNdissent1.17" href="#FRdissent1.17">17</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Chief Justice</small></span> says that <em>Buckley</em> v. <em>Valeo</em>, 424 U.&nbsp;S. 1 (1976) <em>(per curiam)</em>, "already rejected" any test that calls for an assessment of the intent and effect of corporate electioneering.  <em>Ante</em>, at 13.  The "reject[ion]" to which <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Chief Justice</small></span> presumably refers is <em>Buckley</em>'s quotation of <em>Thomas</em> v. <em>Collins</em>, 323 U.&nbsp;S. 516 (1945), where we found impermissibly vague a statute that permitted a union leader to "&nbsp;'laud unionism'&nbsp;" but forbade him to "imply an invitation" to join a union.  <em>Id.</em>, at 534.  The problem with this predicament, we reasoned, was the lack of a clearly permissible opportunity for expression:  Whether words "designed to fall short of invitation would miss that mark is a question both of intent and of effect," and no speaker "safely could assume that anything he might say ... would not be understood by some as an invitation."  <em>Id.</em>, at 535.  We then specified that the speaker in <em>Thomas </em>was left with an impermissibly limited universe of "three choices: (1) to stand on his right and speak freely; (2) to quit, refusing entirely to speak; (3) to trim, and even thus to risk the penalty."  <em>Id.</em>, at 536.</p>
<p>

<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Chief Justice</small></span> implies that considering the intent and effect of corporate advertising during as-applied challenges to 203 would put corporations in precisely the same bind; thus, he wonders how <em>McConnell</em> could use the language of intent and effect without "even address[ing] what <em>Buckley</em>" (and by extension, <em>Thomas</em>) "had to say on the subject."  <em>Ante</em>, at 14.  But one need not look far in our <em>McConnell</em> opinion to understand why we thought that corporations have more than the constrained set of options available to the union leader in <em>Thomas</em>.  Just a few sentences after holding that ads with electioneering intent and effect are regulable, we gave this explanation: "in the future corporations and unions may finance genuine issue ads [shortly before an election] by simply avoiding any specific reference to federal candidates, or in doubtful cases by paying for the ad from a segregated fund."  540 U.&nbsp;S., at 206.  In other words, corporations can find refuge in constitutionally sufficient and clearly delineated safe harbors by modifying the content of their ads (by omitting a candidate's name) or by altering the sources of their ads' financing (from general treasuries to PACs).  <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Chief Justice</small></span> thus wrongly jettisons our conclusions about the constitutionality of regulating ads with electioneering purpose; we meant what we said in <em>McConnell</em>, and we did not overlook First Amendment jurisprudence when we said it.  Whereas <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Chief Justice</small></span> says that BCRA "should provide a safe harbor for those who wish to exercise First Amendment rights," <em>ante</em>, at 14, we already held in <em>McConnell</em> that the campaign finance law accomplishes precisely that.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 18" name="FNdissent1.18" href="#FRdissent1.18">18</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The Chief Justice</small></span> speculates that <em>McConnell</em> derived its test for functional equivalence from "[t]wo key studies," <em>ante</em>, at 12, but not a shred of language in <em>McConnell</em> supports that theory.  In stating the legal standard, <em>McConnell</em> made no mention of any study.  What is the authority, then, for asserting that the studies were pivotal to the standard we announced in <em>McConnell?</em>  See <em>ante</em>, at 12.  Other than WRTL's brief, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Chief Justice</small></span> cites only Judge Henderson's separate district court opinion in <em>McConnell</em>.  <em>Ante</em>, at 12.  But <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Chief Justice </small></span>quotes one part of Judge Henderson's analysis and neglects to mention that she in turn was quoting the lead author of one of the studies in question: "According to the Brennan Center, the <em>Buying Time</em> reports were 'the central piece of evidence marshaled by defenders of' BCRA's electioneering communication provisions 'in support of their constitutional validity.'&nbsp;"  <em>McConnell</em>, 251 F.&nbsp;Supp. 2d, at 307-308 (opinion of Henderson, J.) (italics in original) (quoting deposition of Craig P. Holman, principal co-author of Buying Time 2000: Television Advertising in the 2000 Federal Elections (Brennan Center 2001)).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 19" name="FNdissent1.19" href="#FRdissent1.19">19</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Like the District Court, the only bit of context <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Chief Justice</small></span> would allow the reasonable listener is the congressional agenda: whether the "&nbsp;'issue'&nbsp;" addressed in an ad currently is, or soon will be, "&nbsp;'the subject of legislative scrutiny.'&nbsp;"  <em>Ante</em>, at 20 (quoting 466 F.&nbsp;Supp. 2d, at 207).  For example, T<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>he</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Chief Justice</small></span> says, there would have been "no reason" to think that WRTL's ad constituted anything but a pure issue ad if it addressed a bill pending during Senator Feingold's reelection campaign, such as the Universal National Service Act.  <em>Ante</em>, at 17.  It is revealing, of course, that T<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>he</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Chief Justice</small></span> does not invoke the filibuster issue, the subject of WRTL's ads, as the legislative matter with particular salience during the 2004 election.  But why the reasonable listener can look to Congress but not the calendar on the wall or a WRTL website is difficult to fathom.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 20" name="FNdissent1.20" href="#FRdissent1.20">20</a></strong><br><p><span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Scalia</small></span> also adopts the same misconception that 203 is a "ban" on speech.  See <em>ante</em>, at 18 ("Section 203's line is bright, but it bans vast amounts of political advocacy indistinguishable from hitherto protected speech").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 21" name="FNdissent1.21" href="#FRdissent1.21">21</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;These as-applied challenges provide no reason to second-guess our conclusion in <em>McConnell</em> that the rule for differentiating between electioneering ads and genuine issue ads is administrable.  WRTL's ads clearly have an electioneering purpose and, as explained above, fall comfortably within the heartland of electioneering communications that 203 may validly regulate.  Thus, although <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Scalia</small></span> claims that "[t]oday's cases make it apparent" that <em>McConnell</em> must be overruled, <em>ante</em>, at 18, there is nothing about today's cases that suggests that <em>McConnell</em> is unworkable.  We therefore have no occasion to reconsider <em>McConnell</em> from first principles.</p>
 
        <p></p>
        <p></p>
    </div>

</div>

<div class="caseInformation section">

    <div class="case-information">
        <h2 class="case-information__title"> FEDERAL ELECTION COMMISSION <em>v</em>. WISCONSIN RIGHT TO LIFE, INC., 551 U.S. 449 (2007)</h2>
        <p class="case-information__paragraph"><b class="case-information__paragraph-topic">Citation:</b> 551 U.S. 449</p>
        <p class="case-information__paragraph"><b class="case-information__paragraph-topic">Docket No:</b> No. 06-969</p>
        
        
        <p class="case-information__paragraph"><b class="case-information__paragraph-topic">Argued:</b> April 25, 2007</p>
        <p class="case-information__paragraph"><b class="case-information__paragraph-topic">Decided:</b> June 25, 2007</p>
        <p class="case-information__paragraph"><b class="case-information__paragraph-topic">Court:</b> United States Supreme Court</p>
    </div>

_satellite.ads = {};

_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory = function(nameString, href) {
  // TODO remove this once the correct PA is being used for the lid
  if( _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context') && _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa ) {
    nameString = nameString.replace(/pa:[0-9]+/, "pa:"+_satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa);  
  }
  _satellite.logger.debug('_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory nameString: '+nameString);

  // sample name: "&lid=clk:fs/prod:dts/cid:5088127/msa:259/pa:1313/sub:3107462/pos:1"
  var s = nameString;
  s = s.substr(5);
  var lid = s;
  s = s.replaceAll(':','":"');
  s = s.replaceAll('/','","');
  s = '{"'+s+'"}'
  var adInfo = JSON.parse(s);
  adInfo.lid = lid;
  adInfo.paid = (typeof adInfo.sub != 'undefined');
  adInfo.adId = (adInfo.paid ? adInfo.sub : adInfo.cid);
  var map = {
    'ph':'phone',
    'fs':'website',
    'pv':'profile',
    'cu':'contact',
    'vr':'review-read',
    'wr':'review-write',
    'sl':'super-lawyers'
  };  
  adInfo.type = map[adInfo.clk];   
  adInfo.transactionId = _satellite.getVar('fl_user_id')+'-'+adInfo.adId+'-'+(new Date().getTime());
  
  if( href && adInfo.type == 'phone' ) {
    adInfo.detail = href.replace(/[^\d]/g,'');
    if( adInfo.detail.length > 10 ) {
      adInfo.detail = adInfo.detail.substr(adInfo.detail.length-10);
    }
  }  
  
  return adInfo;
}

_satellite.ads.trackAdEngagement = function(adInfo) {
  if( typeof adInfo.adId == 'undefined' ) {
    return false;
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads = {};
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements = [];
  }
  for( var i=0; i < FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.length; i++ ) {
    if( FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].type == adInfo.type && FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].adId == adInfo.adId ) {
      return true;
    }
  }

  FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.push(adInfo);
  return false;
}

function setTrackerCookie(name, value)
{
  var hostParts = location.host.split('.').reverse();
  var cookieDomain = '.' + hostParts[1] + '.' + hostParts[0];
  document.cookie = name + '=' + value + ';domain=' + cookieDomain + ';path=/';
}

window.addEventListener("load", function(event) {
  try {

    _satellite.trackSearchSource = function(type){
      let toStore = type;
      if(type.indexOf("Top-Nav")>-1 || type.indexOf("Next Steps")>-1 ){
        const pageType = _satellite.getVar("page_page_type");
        if(pageType == "article") toStore += " Article";
        if(pageType == "index") toStore += " Index";
      }
      var practiceCode = _satellite.getVar("practice_code");
      if (practiceCode) {
        toStore+= "|"+practiceCode;
      }
      _satellite.logger.info('s_dsource',toStore);
      _satellite.writeCrossSubdomainCookie("s_dsource", toStore);
    };

    _satellite.slowDown = function(e, goTo){
      e.preventDefault(); // prevent default anchor behavior
      setTimeout(function() {
        window.location = goTo;
      }, 300);
    }
    
    //CTA Search - enter & click
    const nextStepsForm = document.querySelector('.nextSteps form');
    _satellite.logger.info('nextStepsForm',nextStepsForm);
    if (nextStepsForm) {
          nextStepsForm.addEventListener('submit', function(e) {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Next Steps");
        });
    }


    //Top Nav & Find a Lawyer Page - Enter
    const searchComponents = document.querySelectorAll('#search-component,.hero-content form, .search-secondary form');
    _satellite.logger.info('searchComponents',searchComponents);
    searchComponents.forEach((component) => {
      component.addEventListener('keypress', function(e) {
        if (e.key === 'Enter') {
          if (location.host + location.pathname == "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
            _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
            _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Find a Lawyer');
          } else {
              _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
              _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Top-Nav');
          }
        }
      });
    })
    

    //Top Nav & Find a Lawyer Page - click
    const submitButtons = document.querySelectorAll('submit-button, .hero-content input[type="submit"], .search-secondary input[type="submit"]')
    submitButtons.forEach((button) => {
      _satellite.logger.info('button',button);
      button.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        if (location.host + location.pathname === "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Find a Lawyer');
        } else {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Top-Nav');
        }
      });
    })

    //Homepage search
    if (location.host + location.pathname === "www.findlaw.com/") {
      const forms = document.querySelectorAll("form[action*='stdSearch_process.jsp']");
      _satellite.logger.info('forms',forms);
      forms.forEach((form) => {
        _satellite.logger.info('form',form);
        form.addEventListener('submit', function() {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Home");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Home');
        });
      });
    }

    //CTA
    const links = document.querySelectorAll('a[href*="fli="], .articleHeading p a[href*="lawyers.findlaw.com"]');
    _satellite.logger.info('links',links);
    links.forEach((link) => {
      link.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        const goTo = link.getAttribute('href');
        _satellite.slowDown(e, goTo);
        let source = link.dataset.searchLocation || '';
        if (!source) {
          if (link.closest('.cta-public')) {
            source = 'CTA: Banner';
          } else {
            source = 'CTA: Inline Link';
          }
          _satellite.logger.info('source',source);
          _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
        }
      });
    });

    //Right Rail
    const sidebarLawyerSearch = document.querySelector('.sidebarLawyerSearch');
    _satellite.logger.info('sidebarLawyerSearch',sidebarLawyerSearch);
    if (sidebarLawyerSearch) {
          sidebarLawyerSearch.addEventListener('click', function(event) {
            if (event.target.getAttribute('href') && event.target.getAttribute('href').includes('lawyers.findlaw.com')) {
              const goTo = event.target.getAttribute('href');
              _satellite.slowDown(event, goTo);
              _satellite.trackSearchSource("Link Module: Right Rail");
            }
          });
    }

    //Browse
    if (location.host + location.pathname == "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
      const lawyerLinks = document.querySelectorAll('a[href*="/lawyer"]');
      _satellite.logger.info('lawyerLinks',lawyerLinks);
      lawyersLinks.forEach((link) => {
        link.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
          const goTo = this.getAttribute('href');
          _satellite.slowDown(e, goTo);
          let source = "";
          if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/statepractice/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: Popular Cities";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/state/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: State";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/statecounty/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: Top Counties";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/practice/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: Legal Issue";
          }
          _satellite.logger.info('source',source);
          _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
        });
      });
    }
    
    //data-search-location
    const linksWithSearchLocation = document.querySelectorAll('[data-search-location] a, a[data-search-location]');
    _satellite.logger.info('linksWithSearchLocation',linksWithSearchLocation);

        _satellite.logger.info('source',source);
        _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
      });
